23.11.18 08:27:34*** Join: aoeu (2a72255b@gateway/web/freenode/ip.42.114.37.91, aoeu: 42.114.37.91 - http://webchat.freenode.net)
23.11.18 08:27:57<aoeu> how do i describe non static facts in RDF?
23.11.18 08:28:30<aoeu> like things that change over time
23.11.18 08:28:48<aoeu> i don't know how to refer to a triple
23.11.18 08:29:20<aoeu> i'd like to somehow be able to describe who endorses the value of a triple and at what time
23.11.18 08:35:06<darkling> Well, there's RDF reification, or you can use named graphs to attach information to specific clusters of triples.
23.11.18 08:36:54<darkling> There's a other approaches, like splitting your original triple into two, with :a :prop :b becoming :a :prop1 :x. :x :prop2 :b, whence you can annotate :x (which is an entity effectively representing the original triple)
23.11.18 08:37:06<darkling> PROV-O uses the latter approach.
23.11.18 08:39:43<aoeu> that seems complicated
23.11.18 08:39:58<aoeu> as if they didn't think about it when they designed RDF...
23.11.18 08:39:59<darkling> Well, it's no more triples than the other approaches.
23.11.18 08:40:58<darkling> Bear in mind that description logics, which are the mathematical underpinnings of RDF, are also known to be poor at dealing with temporally-changing facts.
23.11.18 08:42:11<darkling> It's never been an easy thing to deal with in RDF, though.
23.11.18 08:42:26<aoeu> that's ridiculous tbh
23.11.18 08:43:34<aoeu> almost makes me want to drop RDF
23.11.18 08:44:14<aoeu> can't triples have an ID?
23.11.18 08:44:32<darkling> Well, that makes them quads, which is what most triplestores actually store these days.
23.11.18 08:44:55<aoeu> the 4th part being context/document/origin?
23.11.18 08:45:00<darkling> The fourth thing in the quad is sometimes known as the context, and it's usually used to put the triples into a named graph.
23.11.18 08:45:51<aoeu> ?
23.11.18 08:46:11<darkling> The fourth item is the URI of the graph that the triple is in.
23.11.18 08:46:50<darkling> If you wanted to, you could probably put every triple into its own graph, but I suspect that most triplestores would have trouble delivering usable performance if you do that.
23.11.18 08:46:54<darkling> (I might be wrong, though)
                 <aoeu> how would i describe ((john hasAge 10) at 10/10/2010)
23.11.18 08:47:41<darkling> Urgh. Generally, you'd describe :john :hasBirthDate 2000-10-10"
23.11.18 08:48:01<aoeu> right, but i needed an example with temporal data
23.11.18 08:48:27<aoeu> John livesIn Canada
23.11.18 08:48:40<aoeu> John livesIn Australia
23.11.18 08:48:47<aoeu> with the measurement date
23.11.18 08:49:26<aoeu> ideally, ever fact would be supported by a witness and a timestamp
23.11.18 08:49:30<darkling> You'd put those in graphs of their own, and then annotate each graph with a temporal validity range. :G1 :valid-to "2000-10-10". :G2 :valid-from "2000-10-11".
23.11.18 08:49:43<darkling> Then you can attach the witnesses and timestamps to the graph as well.
23.11.18 08:50:24<aoeu> where do graph ids come from?
23.11.18 08:50:29<darkling> Typically, you'd find there's more than one triple in each such event -- change of address and phone number, for example, would go along with that.
23.11.18 08:50:45<darkling> You invent the graph ID, same as you invent the IDs of your other resources.
23.11.18 08:51:02<aoeu> no, the timestamp would always be different
23.11.18 08:51:14<aoeu> let's imagine a GPS or IoT device capturing temperature or location
23.11.18 08:51:18<aoeu> every 1 second
23.11.18 08:56:57<aoeu> is that not a common use case?
23.11.18 08:57:41<darkling> Well, in that case, I'd have a resource which is a measurement, and then hang the value, unit, timestamp, and other index or annotation values for that measurement off that resource.
23.11.18 08:59:32<darkling> That's basically the "resource in the middle" model I described at the outset.
23.11.18 09:12:29<aoeu> that doesn't sound right

02.12.18 17:56:04<sbp> Anchakor: content could be signed for its authority and distributed via any means. Prof Bernstein advocated a search engine for such information, and the most well known real system that bears any resemblance is the PGP web of trust, seldom used in practice outside of package distribution
02.12.18 17:56:54<sbp> I tried adding a web of trust mechanism to the web stack, but a Google representative pretended that he did not understand the use cases (which I explained in great detail) and barred uptake of the idea. decentralisation is not on Google's menu
02.12.18 17:58:46<sbp> I more or less agree with your core thesis, but the more complex the system the more difficult it is to achieve these things. I believe we have technologies now to help us fix those problems but, like decentralisation, they are in an extremely early stage of development
02.12.18 17:59:24<sbp> if you have read timbl's original design issues about the Semantic Web, you will find that he was thinking about this a lot: about migration of data, self-healing, those sorts of things. things that seem almost magic now, given the rot that we have to put up with
02.12.18 18:05:12<Anchakor> I think the main point of semweb is machine-automated interoperability
02.12.18 18:07:36<Anchakor> the interoperability of ideally all the things - data, information, identifiers, protocols (authorization, authentication...) etc.
02.12.18 18:09:13<Anchakor> eventually the decentralized community will have to use/reinvent something like semweb as there are way too many non-interoperable P2P ddistributed protocols etc.
02.12.18 18:10:05<sbp> maybe that's true in general, but there were many other ideas going on in there. for example https://www.w3.org/DesignIssues/PaperTrail.html would read to a modern reader much like a manifesto for smart contracts, but of course you will discern that the motivations and framing were somewhat (if not entirely) different
02.12.18 18:10:51<sbp> but on your use or reinvention point, I think you are much more optimistic than I am
02.12.18 18:11:21<sbp> obvious, basic, low-hanging-fruits in technology have a strange habit of not being plucked
02.12.18 18:11:51<sbp> well, in computing I should say. it's not so bad in technology as a wider field
02.12.18 18:12:07<sbp> although computing is rapidly eating all of the rest of technology
02.12.18 18:12:38<Anchakor> I don't follow, systems of high-order interoperability are by no means low-hanging fruit
02.12.18 18:13:39<sbp> then perhaps we agree after all :-)
02.12.18 18:14:13<Anchakor> it's evidenced by the current state of the semweb stack
02.12.18 18:15:19<Anchakor> there have been made sacrifices to keep stuff simple and practical, even internally, not just on user-interface
02.12.18 18:15:58<Anchakor> turns out that kind of simplicity is limiting
02.12.18 18:16:00<sbp> I think it's low-hanging fruit because it's an obvious idea, and I think it's feasible because it's not as difficult as blasting man to the moon, which we achieved in 1969. p2p systems are old, and they were already buzzworded around 2003 or so, which is when the Semantic Web ideas were around their peak too
02.12.18 18:16:38<sbp> "simple" and "practical" are not words I would associate with any part of the RDF stack
02.12.18 18:17:24<sbp> OWL species checkers, SPARQL parsers... I hope you have not had the mispleasure of implementing these things
02.12.18 18:17:55<sbp> (in other words it is fun, if you're a masochist)
02.12.18 18:18:37<Anchakor> see, I think in order to work best, it should internally be even more complex and large, to handle all the complexities of real work, but with clear and simple user interfaces for use cases
02.12.18 18:18:57<Anchakor> s/real work/real world/
02.12.18 18:19:36<sbp> yeah, but that would be useful complexity. I agree, but I think that effectively they crammed so much *useless* complexity into RDF as to make it otiose and unbearable to work with. indeed the whole thread today is predicated on that observation
02.12.18 18:19:51<Anchakor> agreed
02.12.18 18:20:12<sbp> and I think the flavour of the day is to try to remove that useless complexity. what I was arguing with dbooth earlier is that we need to kinda add the sort of complexity that you're talking about, the useful complexity, back in
02.12.18 18:20:33<sbp> otherwise the whole endeavour is somewhat bland and pointless in my opinion
02.12.18 18:20:53<sbp> though I could feign ambivalence for the sake of social harmony
02.12.18 18:21:34<Anchakor> the sacrifices I meant are like the Linked Data identifier thing, which says use HTTP, but IPFS or other protocols are becoming valid choices and identifier lifecycle management should be well studied and handled thing
02.12.18 18:22:25<sbp> I don't know, some people are turning to gopher instead

06.12.18 16:52:58<AAA_awright> One of the messages on the mailing list suggests that bnodes are guarenteed to only exist within the same graph; they aren't subject to open world principle in the same manner that URIs are (the number of instances of that bnode in the universe is guarenteed)
06.12.18 16:53:26<AAA_awright> Is that supposed to be a feature of RDF, or is this just a side effect of how we serialize graphs?
06.12.18 16:56:25<ww> well, the "open world" says "there's always more to be said about some subject"
06.12.18 16:56:33<ww> that's true of bnodes or uris
06.12.18 16:56:44<ww> but with bnodes, you just can't be sure it's the same subject
06.12.18 16:58:05<ww> well, sometimes you can, if you can figure out that they refer to the same thing using some logic rule
06.12.18 17:00:37*** Join: shellac (~textual@cpc89568-aztw32-2-0-cust775.18-1.cable.virginm.net, shellac: Textual User)
06.12.18 17:07:55<Anchakor> I think it's just a side effect of not having a serialization with global (IRI?) bnode labels
06.12.18 17:08:14<AAA_awright> ww: Well yeah, that's true of _resources_ and bnodes aren't resources, they're existential quantifiers for resources.
06.12.18 17:09:09<AAA_awright> But if I have a graph like { _:x a Car . } you know that bnode inside won't ever have an additional statement extending that existential quantifier
06.12.18 17:10:26<darkling> So, "there exists an x which is a car, but we can't say anything more about it here"
06.12.18 17:11:17<Anchakor> I would argue there is no changing of bnodes, if you add a statement regarding a bnode, the old bnode ceases to exists and is replaced by a new one - a new different existential quantifier

06.12.18 17:29:35<AAA_awright> Well for things like linked lists, what if I want to replace item n in a linked list? I don't believe that's possible to do in SPARQL if the linked list is a bnode
06.12.18 17:30:02<bjonnh> sure but if your purpose was to do that, why use bnodes in the first place
06.12.18 17:30:03<AAA_awright> But a urn:uuid: URI seems ideal for assigning addresses to items in the list, similar to how computers have addresses in memory
06.12.18 17:30:25<AAA_awright> bjonnh: Because that's how the list syntax is defined to work
06.12.18 17:31:09<AAA_awright> The only issue is serialization, if you serialize the graph you have to serialize the addresses (the uuid urn)
06.12.18 17:31:42<AAA_awright> Now you could invent a new syntax for that... it wouldn't be pretty, maybe something like
06.12.18 17:32:51<AAA_awright> Well, let's invent a new media type that can do this: ( address item, address item , ... )
06.12.18 17:33:13<AAA_awright> or rather  ( address0 item0, address1 item1 , ..., addressn item n )

                 <AAA_awright> address0 rdf:first item0; rdf:rest address1 .

18.01.19 19:44:14*** Join: AAA_awright (~aaa@2607:5300:60:7d4::1, AAA_awright: A Cubed A Right)
18.01.19 19:50:59<AAA_awright> Hey SWIG, I've got an interesting case where I've got a data graph, and for Reasons the IRI of the resource might become lost and so I mint a bnode instead.
                 <AAA_awright> Presumably this is legal, the bnode is correctly asserting that "some resource with these properties exist".
                 <AAA_awright> But is there a function that ensures the data is constistent? If I test for graph isomorphism, should the bnode still match an IRI?
18.01.19 19:51:37<darkling> No, graph isomorphism will only match bnodes to bnodes.
18.01.19 19:52:11<darkling> Named resources only match resources of the same name, not bnodes.
18.01.19 19:53:42<AAA_awright> So then my isomorphism test is running correctly; but then the follow up is what sort of function can test that my derivitive graph is consistent with the source data?
18.01.19 19:56:03<darkling> Without modifying the isomorphism code, you're probably looking at a really brain-dead algorithm,
18.01.19 19:56:30<darkling> where you assign candidate IRIs from the reference graph to your bnodes and test for isomorphism,
18.01.19 19:56:58<darkling> then try a different set of assignments, rinse, repeat until you run out of possible assignments or you find a match.
18.01.19 19:57:42<darkling> However, it should be possible to take a "proper" RDF isomorphism algorithm and modify it to do what you want.
18.01.19 19:58:57<darkling> Sadly, the few available papers on RDF graph isomorphism algorithms (that I managed to find) are actually quite hard to read and get a working algorithm out of.
18.01.19 20:02:32*** Quit: mischat (~mischat@2a02:c7f:c01b:ef00:109e:e9cc:9346:df5f) left #swig: Remote host closed the connection
18.01.19 20:03:20<AAA_awright> Here's the pretty stupid algorithm I use for isomprohism here https://github.com/awwright/node-rdf/blob/master/lib/GraphEquals.js
18.01.19 20:04:05<darkling> Ah, you're not using someone else's.
18.01.19 20:04:10<darkling> That probably helps in this case. :)
18.01.19 20:05:22<darkling> It's a bit late in the day (or early in the morning) for me to look at the detail right now.
18.01.19 20:05:32<AAA_awright> heheh
18.01.19 20:05:35<AAA_awright> Any known cases in the wild of implementations losing the IRI of a resource? I imagine lots of people would get tripped up on that
18.01.19 20:06:01<AAA_awright> Specifically what's going on here is I'm querying the information and making it available in RDFa, but I have to handle the case where there's no about= attribute in the output
18.01.19 20:06:45<AAA_awright> And so in that case, the statements get pulled from the database, but the actual subject of the resource never makes it into the result
18.01.19 20:06:58<AAA_awright> Hence it's parsed as a bnode in RDFa
18.01.19 20:07:02<darkling> I'm not aware of any, but there's a great deal I'm not aware of. :)
18.01.19 20:08:41<darkling> Here's my graph isomorphism algorithm implementation: http://git.darksatanic.net/cgi/gitweb.cgi?p=lagra.git;a=blob;f=src/lagra.erl;h=fa34f52adc228cdf090c9438e703d9d19d33a5ad;hb=1c53c431701ff2cf382b3b9eb610dc60acfb394f#l101
18.01.19 20:09:26<darkling> It's pretty much from there all the way down to the bottom.
18.01.19 20:09:29<AAA_awright> Nice!
18.01.19 20:10:40<darkling> Ooh, here's an idea.
18.01.19 20:11:13<darkling> Compare the list of IRIs in the reference graph with the list of IRIs in the current graph (with the extra bnodes).
18.01.19 20:11:28<darkling> If there are IRIs in the current graph that aren't in the reference, it's not a match.
18.01.19 20:11:47<darkling> If there are IRIs in the reference that aren't in the current, modify them to be bnodes.
18.01.19 20:11:58<darkling> *Then* run a standard isomorphism test on it.
18.01.19 20:15:17<AAA_awright> The number of peopleon the entire planet who understand how bnodes actually work I could probably fit into a standard bedroom
18.01.19 20:15:40<darkling> You could probably fit them into a fridge. :)
18.01.19 20:16:20<AAA_awright> That's not a bad idea, darkling
18.01.19 20:16:46<darkling> What, fitting all the people who understand bnodes into a fridge? ;)
18.01.19 20:17:12<AAA_awright> hahaha well that too
18.01.19 20:17:49<darkling> But yes, I think it'll do the job you want.
18.01.19 20:19:20<darkling> RDF has a peculiarly nice but non-trivial behaviour with graph isomorphism.
18.01.19 20:20:15<darkling> If your graphs have all vertices uniquely identifiable, then isomorphism is trivial, because you can just compare the identified nodes and the arcs between them.
18.01.19 20:20:32<darkling> That's basically RDF with no bnodes: is the set of triples the same in both graphs?
18.01.19 20:21:18<darkling> If you have all anonymous vertices, then it's appallingly difficult (more or less, the obvious O(n!) algorithm).
18.01.19 20:21:48<darkling> RDF tends to have _mostly_ identified vertices, plus a few anonymous ones.
18.01.19 20:22:32<darkling> So you start out by doing the easy parts: count the vertices, count the triples, compare the vertex labels (IRIs), compare the set of triples without bnodes.
18.01.19 20:23:33<AAA_awright> There's also the issue that my output graph might be a subset of the data (actually, usually it will be) — I'm thinking I'll just create a new function called "constistent with" or something, that all the data expressed in some subset is present in the reference set
18.01.19 20:23:53<darkling> Urgh. OK, that's a different thing again.
18.01.19 20:24:16<darkling> You're looking for the embeddings of your graph in the reference.
18.01.19 20:24:45<darkling> Oh, again, that's probably fairly easy.
18.01.19 20:24:53<darkling> SPARQL will do it for you.
18.01.19 20:25:06<AAA_awright> Right now I'm testing that the output hasn't modified the source data in some undesirable way, i.e. change the datatype of a string, or turned a URI into a string, stuff like that
18.01.19 20:25:20<darkling> Write your graph as a SPARQL query, where the bnodes are variables, and the IRIs are themselves.
18.01.19 20:25:25<AAA_awright> but I'm guessing turning a URI into a bnode is OK
18.01.19 20:25:28<darkling> Then the SPARQL engine will find the embeddings.
18.01.19 20:27:13<darkling> So, given :a :p _:1. _:1 :q :b., you'd write SPARQL to find :a :p ?x. ?x :q :b.
18.01.19 20:28:06<AAA_awright> Yeah
18.01.19 20:29:07<AAA_awright> I'll run this by the mailing list
18.01.19 20:29:32<darkling> The only thing is, if something _has_ done one of the Bad Things you mentioned above, you don't get diagnostics, just "doesn't exist here".
18.01.19 20:30:25<darkling> You may want to have a set of alternative relaxations of the query to find which things have gone wrong.
18.01.19 20:30:51<darkling> (i.e., replace all literals with a variable, or replace each IRI with a variable in turn)
18.01.19 20:32:33<darkling> I think I'm going to have to go and do a relaxation of myself at this point.
18.01.19 20:32:46<darkling> Probably under a duvet somewhere upstairs for 8 hours. :)
18.01.19 20:32:51<darkling> G'night.
18.01.19 20:48:56<AAA_awright> Thanks darkling!

12.02.19 02:08:29*** Join: mayero (~mayero@196.207.143.242, mayero: realname)
12.02.19 02:12:00<mayero> Hello SWIG Members, I am new to the semantic web and would love some assistance in the creation of ontologies.I have three main questions and would really appreciate any assistance given.
12.02.19 02:12:42<mayero> First questions,what are dynamic ontologies and how are they created?
12.02.19 02:16:43<mayero> Second question,say I have an ontology that shows a phone call with a caller and callee and their geolocations,how can I make the caller and callee have different IRU's without them being recognized as being similar?
12.02.19 02:17:35<mayero> Last question, is there a tool that dynamically maps csv data to already created ontologies?
12.02.19 02:18:06<mayero> Thank you .I appreciate any response and help I can get.
12.02.19 02:33:26*** Quit: mayero (~mayero@196.207.143.242) left #swig: Read error: Connection reset by peer
12.02.19 02:36:39*** Join: mayero (~mayero@196.207.143.242, mayero: realname)
12.02.19 03:13:04<darkling> Well, the last question is probably simplest. There's a couple of things that will turn tabular data into RDF.
12.02.19 03:13:47<darkling> The main one is R2RML, which is an RDF language for querying SQL databases and generating RDF from them.
12.02.19 03:14:42<darkling> With the right implementation and choice of database, you can go straight from CSV. (The H2 database, for example, can use a CSV file as a "table").
12.02.19 03:15:13<darkling> Good implementations of R2RML aren't common, though, and you may find it hard to track one down.
12.02.19 03:16:16<darkling> There's also D2RQ, which may help.
12.02.19 03:16:17*** Quit: mayero (~mayero@196.207.143.242) left #swig: Read error: Connection reset by peer
12.02.19 03:26:38<Anchakor> there is also OpenRefine (formerly Google Refine) and this extension https://github.com/stkenny/grefine-rdf-extension
12.02.19 03:27:08<darkling> Oh yes, I forgot about that one.
12.02.19 03:27:17<darkling> Never used it in anger, which is probably why.
12.02.19 03:28:14<Anchakor> http://openrefine.org/
12.02.19 03:32:09*** Join: jmvanel (~jmv@2a01:e35:8a9e:4b30:d81d:e27d:ffab:a0c2, jmvanel: JM Vanel)
12.02.19 03:33:03<Anchakor> "dynamic ontologies" is not a common term, it may refer just to a challenge of evolving ontologies, or differently to crowdsource-created ontologies
12.02.19 03:33:50<Anchakor> and I don't understand the second question

15.02.19 17:57:15<Poka> well since it appears nobody talks in here anymore, i'll just be rambling, because all this gitter/slack stuff is way too bloated. thoguh it looks like Tim is finally working on some kind of bridge to solid-websoket stuff (and there's probably stil abandonware on MElvin's githubs somewhere) https://github.com/solid/solid-gitter/commit/72531b6d197493d3851a89a5fc6bd0853a2b6b3f
15.02.19 17:58:25<Poka> no idea what sort of delays are involved in 'eventual consistency' and consensus re IPLD CRDTs distributing RDF snippets of our messages around w/o freenode. havent really even begun reading that constantly-evolving mountain of specs.. https://github.com/ipfs/dynamic-data-and-capabilities/issues/3
15.02.19 17:59:36<Poka> Matrix allows arbitrary JSON bodies, so it's fairly trivial to adapt it to sending aroudn JSON-LD of messages marked up in SIOC as RDF
15.02.19 18:00:15<Poka> the main issue seems to be the overengineering of it or maybe it's just that the only usable implementatiion is Python. requiring a gig of ram for a basic chatserver probably not even including teh other kafka/whatever stuff is ridiculous
15.02.19 18:00:36<Poka> reinvntion of various authy things that ought to be more SOLID/something standard
15.02.19 18:00:39<Poka> but otherwise a nice effort
15.02.19 18:01:47<Poka> it looks like IPLD has at least some venture backing. presumably as a sort of RedHAt'y play where the core products are open implementations of open standards.. https://protocol.ai/projects/#IPLD
15.02.19 18:02:00<Poka> as a dilettante and not a real hacker i'll just be watching this from afar rather than joining them
15.02.19 18:02:56<Poka> what's still underplayed vs these clean room engineer the whole idealist post-blockchain/eth universe at once
15.02.19 18:03:19<Poka> is incremental improvements. NNTP style 'peering' of solid-daemon nodes so public updates just appear everywhere almost instantly

19.02.19 15:15:07<Poka> and 'slash URIs' rather than anchors within a vocab doc

19.02.19 15:19:48<Poka> whatever their ideas are for philsophical purity, doing what theyre doing is goign to be  alot more complicated for data consumers. they probably should stop.
